```
<ai_inventor_context>
<ai_inventor_summary>
You are one of many LLMs in AI Inventor — an automated research system that generates NOVEL and FEASIBLE hypotheses, investigates them through experiments and research, and produces a paper.

Your output feeds other LLMs downstream. This demands your ABSOLUTE MAXIMUM reasoning — every output must be deeply thought out and maximally useful. Surface-level responses waste downstream computation.
</ai_inventor_summary>

<your_role>
YOU ARE: An artifact executor (Step 3.3: GEN_ART in the invention loop)

Executing a plan to produce a concrete artifact.
GEN_PAPER_TEXT will use your artifact in the next paper draft.

Rigorous artifact with clear results → strong paper. Sloppy artifact → misdirected research.
</your_role>
</ai_inventor_context>

<task>
Conduct thorough, unbiased research on the given topic.
Adapt your investigation approach based on the research question and domain.
</task>

<available_tools>
Web research is available through the aii-web-tools skill, in three levels (broad → specific):

1. web search — Returns titles, URLs, snippets. Use first to discover and scan the landscape.
2. web fetch — Reads a page and returns its content as markdown (HTML or PDF). Use to understand a source. May miss specific details — use fetch_grep below if it doesn't find what you need.
3. fetch_grep — Regex search over a page/PDF's full text. Returns exact matching sections with context. Use for precise details, exact numbers, methodology, or PDFs.

Workflow: search → fetch (understand) → fetch_grep (extract specifics).
</available_tools>

<tool_use>
Maximize parallel tool calls. Parallelize independent operations, only sequentialize dependencies.
- Multiple searches/fetches on different topics → parallel in one turn
- Search then fetch results → sequential (need URLs first)
</tool_use>

<critical_requirements>
1. SOURCE DIVERSITY - Consult MANY sources (10+), not just the first few results
2. AVOID SELECTION BIAS - Actively seek contradicting viewpoints, not just confirming ones
3. TRIANGULATE - Cross-reference claims across multiple independent sources
4. ACKNOWLEDGE UNCERTAINTY - Be honest about confidence levels and limitations
5. SYNTHESIZE - Produce a coherent answer that accounts for conflicting evidence
</critical_requirements>

<system_reminder>
Do not ask follow up questions and do not ask the user anything. Execute all steps independently.
You must follow the todo list provided in each prompt exactly as written.
No placeholders, stubs, or incomplete code — all code must be complete and functional.
</system_reminder>

<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>
```

<artifact_plan>
id: gen_plan_research_1_idx1
type: research
title: Research Ricci curvature methods
summary: >-
  Investigate Ollivier-Ricci and Forman-Ricci curvature computation methods, available Python implementations, and their computational
  complexity for citation network analysis.
runpod_compute_profile: cpu_light
question: >-
  What are the available methods and Python implementations for computing Ollivier-Ricci and Forman-Ricci curvature in graphs,
  and which are most suitable for efficient computation on citation networks with thousands of nodes?
research_plan: >-
  Step-by-Step Research Plan. Phase 1: Seminal Papers and Theoretical Foundations. 1. Search for Samal et al. 2018 paper 'Comparative
  analysis of two discretizations of Ricci curvature'. 2. Find Ollivier-Ricci curvature original definition (Ollivier 2009).
  3. Find Forman-Ricci curvature definition (Forman 2003). Phase 2: Python Implementations. 4. Investigate graph-ricci-curvature
  PyPI package. 5. Check GitHub repository: https://github.com/saibhargav2507/graph-ricci-curvature. 6. Search for alternative
  implementations. Phase 3: Computational Complexity. 7. Analyze complexity: Ollivier-Ricci O(N*E), Forman-Ricci O(E). 8.
  Search for approximation methods. Phase 4: Citation Network Datasets. 9. Find dataset statistics (Cora, CiteSeer, PubMed).
  10. Search for prior applications. Phase 5: Synthesis. 11. Create implementation comparison. 12. Provide code template.
  13. Estimate runtime for PubMed dataset.
explanation: >-
  This research is essential for the hypothesis validation because the approach depends entirely on computing curvature discrepancy
  between Ollivier-Ricci and Forman-Ricci curvature. Without understanding available implementations, their computational
  complexity, API usage, and limitations, we cannot design valid experiments. Key risks: 1) No suitable Python implementation
  exists, 2) Complexity is higher than O(N*E), 3) Memory requirements exceed resources. This research mitigates these risks.
</artifact_plan>

<investigation_process>
1. DIVERGE: Brainstorm multiple angles/framings of the question before searching. Think across fields — what adjacent domains might have relevant insights?
2. SEARCH: Multiple queries per angle with different phrasings to discover the landscape
3. FETCH: Read promising URLs at high level. Snippets are NOT enough — fetch full pages
4. DETAIL: aii-web-tools fetch_grep for specifics from key pages/PDFs
5. CONTRAST: Actively try to disprove your emerging conclusions. Search with different phrasings, "[topic] criticism", "[topic] limitations". Check across fields — the same finding may exist under different names
6. SYNTHESIZE: Integrate into balanced conclusion
7. ITERATE: Expect to repeat steps 2-6 if findings are incomplete or one-sided. Don't settle on first results
8. SUMMARIZE: Output JSON must include 'title' and 'summary' fields
</investigation_process>

<output_requirements>
- Write research_out.json to your workspace with all findings
- Provide your finding as clear prose WITH NUMBERED CITATIONS
- EVERY factual claim must have a citation number in brackets: [1], [2], [1, 3], etc.
- Include BOTH supporting AND contradicting evidence
- Be explicit about confidence level and what would change it
- End with follow-up questions for further investigation
</output_requirements>

<repo_upload_exclusions>
Your finished workspace is published to a public GitHub repo. If it will hold files that should NOT be published — content-addressed caches (e.g. a `cache/` directory of thousands of hash-named files), large transient intermediates, model checkpoints, or scratch downloads — list regex patterns for them in the `upload_ignore_regexes` output field. Each pattern is matched against a path RELATIVE to your workspace root in POSIX form (e.g. `(^|/)cache/`, `(^|/)checkpoints/`). They apply on top of the built-in exclusions; leave the field empty if every workspace file should be published. Do NOT use this to hide real deliverables (code, results, datasets the paper relies on) — only genuine cache/scratch bulk.
</repo_upload_exclusions>

Research everything specified in the artifact plan, but you may also investigate additional relevant aspects beyond what's listed. Investigate this question thoroughly.

[Learn more](https://info.arxiv.org/about) \u00d7\n\n[](https://arxiv.org/IgnoreMe) [ ](https://arxiv.org/) [ Search ](https://arxiv.org/search) [Submit](https://arxiv.org/user/create) [Donate](https://info.arxiv.org/about/donate.html) [Log in](https://arxiv.org/login)\n\nSearch arXiv\n\nPress Enter to search * [Advanced search](https://arxiv.org/search/advanced)\n\n# Mathematics > Differential Geometry\n\n**arXiv:1712.07600** (math)\n\n[Submitted on 20 Dec 2017 ([v1](https://arxiv.org/abs/1712.07600v1)), last revised 9 Jun 2018 (this version, v2)]\n\n# Title:Comparative analysis of two discretizations of Ricci curvature for complex networks\n\nAuthors:[Areejit Samal](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=Samal,+A), [R.P. Sreejith](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=R.P.), [Jiao Gu](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=Gu,+J), [Shiping Liu](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=Liu,+S), [Emil Saucan](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=Saucan,+E), [J\u00fcrgen Jost](https://arxiv.org/search/math?searchtype=author&query=Jost,+J)\n\nView a PDF of the paper titled Comparative analysis of two discretizations of Ricci curvature for complex networks, by Areejit Samal and 5 other authors\n\n[View PDF](/pdf/1712.07600)\n\n> Abstract:We have performed an empirical comparison of two distinct notions of discrete Ricci curvature for graphs or networks, namely, the Forman-Ricci curvature and Ollivier-Ricci curvature. Importantly, these two discretizations of the Ricci curvature were developed based on different properties of the classical smooth notion, and thus, the two notions shed light on different aspects of network structure and behavior. Nevertheless, our extensive computational analysis in a wide range of both model and real-world networks shows that the two discretizations of Ricci curvature are highly correlated in many networks. Moreover, we show that if one considers the augmented Forman-Ricci curvature which also accounts for the two-dimensional simplicial complexes arising in graphs, the observed correlation between the two discretizations is even higher, especially, in real networks. Besides the potential theoretical implications of these observations, the close relationship between the two discretizations has practical implications whereby Forman-Ricci curvature can be employed in place of Ollivier-Ricci curvature for faster computation in larger real-world networks whenever coarse analysis suffices.\n\nComments: | Published version. New results added in this version. files\n\n## Repository files navigation\n\n  * README\n  * Apache-2.0 license\n\n\n\nMore items\n\n# GraphRicciCurvature\n\nA Python library to compute Discrete Ricci curvature, Ricci flow, and Ricci community on NetworkX graph.\n\n[](https://colab.research.google.com/github/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/blob/master/notebooks/tutorial.ipynb) [](https://mybinder.org/v2/gh/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/master?filepath=notebooks%2Ftutorial.ipynb) [](https://badge.fury.io/py/GraphRicciCurvature) [](https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/actions) [](https://graphriccicurvature.readthedocs.io/en/latest/?badge=latest) [](https://pepy.tech/project/graphriccicurvature) [](https://opensource.org/licenses/Apache-2.0)\n\n* * *\n\nThis work computes the **Ollivier-Ricci Curvature**[Ni], **Ollivier-Ricci Flow**[Ni2,Ni3], **Forman-Ricci Curvature**(or **Forman curvature**)[Sreejith, Samal], and **Ricci community**[Ni3] detected by Ollivier-Ricci flow metric.\n\n[](https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/raw/master/doc/_static/rf-manifold.png) [](https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/raw/master/doc/_static/karate_demo.png)\n\nCurvature is a geometric property to describe the local shape of an object. If we draw two parallel paths on a surface with positive curvature like a sphere, these two paths move closer to each other while for a negatively curved surface like a saddle, these two paths tend to be apart. Currently there are multiple ways to discretize curvature on graph, in this library, we include two of the most frequently used discrete Ricci curvature: **Ollivier-Ricci curvature** which is based on optimal transportation theory and **Forman-Ricci curvature** which is base on CW complexes.\n\nIn [Ni], edge Ricci curvature is observed to play an important role in the graph structure. An edge with positive curvature represents an edge within a cluster, while a negatively curved edge tent to be a bridge within clusters. Also, negatively curved edges are highly related to graph connectivity, with negatively curved edges removed from a connected graph, the graph soon become disconnected.\n\nRicci flow is a process to uniformized the edge Ricci curvature of the graph. For a given graph, the Ricci flow gives a \"Ricci flow metric\" on each edge as edge weights, such that under these edge weights, the Ricci curvature of the graph is mostly equal everywhere. In [Ni3], this \"Ricci flow metric\" is shown to be able to detect communities.\n\nBoth Ricci curvature and Ricci flow metric can act as a graph fingerprint for graph classification. The different graph gives different edge Ricci curvature distributions and different Ricci flow metric.\n\nVideo demonstration of Ricci flow for community detection:\n\n[ ](https://youtu.be/QlENb_XlJ_8)\n\n## Package Requirement\n\n  * [NetworkX](https://github.com/networkx/networkx) >= 2.0 (Based Graph library)\n  * [NetworKit](https://github.com/kit-parco/networkit) >= 6.1 (Shortest path algorithm)\n  * [NumPy](https://github.com/numpy/numpy) (POT support)\n  * [POT](https://github.com/rflamary/POT) (For optimal transportation distance)\n  * [python-louvain](https://github.com/taynaud/python-louvain) (For faster modularity computation)\n\n\n\n## Installation\n\n### Installing via pip\n\n\n    pip3 install [--user] GraphRicciCurvature\n\n  * From version 0.4.0, NetworKit is required to compute shortest path for density distribution. If the installation of NetworKit failed, please refer to [NetworKit' Installation instructions](https://github.com/networkit/networkit#installation-instructions).\n\n\n\n### Upgrading via pip\n\nTo run with the latest code for the best performance, upgrade GraphRicciCurvature to the latest version with pip:\n\n\n    pip3 install [--user] --upgrade GraphRicciCurvature\n\n## Getting Started\n\n  * Check the jupyter notebook tutorial on [nbviewer](https://nbviewer.jupyter.org/github/saibalmars/GraphRi"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature\" --max-chars 15000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "aefd5039b0fd", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/.oh_sessions/4aa7348867f64e409f0a4d14a0cd32a3/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

GraphRicciCurvature 0.5.3.2\n\npip install GraphRicciCurvature __ Copy PIP instructions\n\n[ Latest release ](/project/GraphRicciCurvature/)\n\nReleased:  Jun 26, 2024\n\nCompute discrete Ricci curvatures and Ricci flow on NetworkX graphs.\n\n### Navigation\n\n  * __Project description\n  * __Release history\n  * __Download files\n\n\n\n###  Verified details __\n\n_These details have been[verified by PyPI](https://docs.pypi.org/project_metadata/#verified-details)_\n\n###### Maintainers\n\n[ saibalmars ](/user/saibalmars/)\n\n### Unverified details\n\n_These details have**not** been verified by PyPI_\n\n###### Project links\n\n  * [ __Homepage](https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature)\n\n\n\n###### Meta\n\n  * **License:** Apache Software License\n  * **Author:** __Project description\n  * __Project details\n  * __Release history\n  * __Download files\n\n\n\n## Project description\n\n# GraphRicciCurvature\n\nA Python library to compute Discrete Ricci curvature, Ricci flow, and Ricci community on NetworkX graph.\n\n[](https://colab.research.google.com/github/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/blob/master/notebooks/tutorial.ipynb) [](https://mybinder.org/v2/gh/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/master?filepath=notebooks%2Ftutorial.ipynb) [](https://badge.fury.io/py/GraphRicciCurvature) [](https://github.com/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/actions) [](https://graphriccicurvature.readthedocs.io/en/latest/?badge=latest) [](https://pepy.tech/project/graphriccicurvature) [](https://opensource.org/licenses/Apache-2.0)\n\n* * *\n\nThis work computes the **Ollivier-Ricci Curvature**[Ni], **Ollivier-Ricci Flow**[Ni2,Ni3], **Forman-Ricci Curvature**(or **Forman curvature**)[Sreejith, Samal], and **Ricci community**[Ni3] detected by Ollivier-Ricci flow metric.\n\nCurvature is a geometric property to describe the local shape of an object. If we draw two parallel paths on a surface with positive curvature like a sphere, these two paths move closer to each other while for a negatively curved surface like a saddle, these two paths tend to be apart. Currently there are multiple ways to discretize curvature on graph, in this library, we include two of the most frequently used discrete Ricci curvature: **Ollivier-Ricci curvature** which is based on optimal transportation theory and **Forman-Ricci curvature** which is base on CW complexes.\n\nIn [Ni], edge Ricci curvature is observed to play an important role in the graph structure. An edge with positive curvature represents an edge within a cluster, while a negatively curved edge tent to be a bridge within clusters. Also, negatively curved edges are highly related to graph connectivity, with negatively curved edges removed from a connected graph, the graph soon become disconnected.\n\nRicci flow is a process to uniformized the edge Ricci curvature of the graph. For a given graph, the Ricci flow gives a \"Ricci flow metric\" on each edge as edge weights, such that under these edge weights, the Ricci curvature of the graph is mostly equal everywhere. In [Ni3], this \"Ricci flow metric\" is shown to be able to detect communities.\n\nBoth Ricci curvature and Ricci flow metric can act as a graph fingerprint for graph classification. The different graph gives different edge Ricci curvature distributions and different Ricci flow metric.\n\nVideo demonstration of Ricci flow for community detection:\n\n[ ](https://youtu.be/QlENb_XlJ_8)\n\n## Package Requirement\n\n  * [NetworkX](https://github.com/networkx/networkx) >= 2.0 (Based Graph library)\n  * [NetworKit](https://github.com/kit-parco/networkit) >= 6.1 (Shortest path algorithm)\n  * [NumPy](https://github.com/numpy/numpy) (POT support)\n  * [POT](https://github.com/rflamary/POT) (For optimal transportation distance)\n  * [python-louvain](https://github.com/taynaud/python-louvain) (For faster modularity computation)\n\n\n\n## Installation\n\n### Installing via pip\n\n\n    pip3 install [--user] GraphRicciCurvature\n\n\n  * From version 0.4.0, NetworKit is required to compute shortest path for density distribution. If the installation of NetworKit failed, please refer to [NetworKit' Installation instructions](https://github.com/networkit/networkit#installation-instructions).\n\n\n\n### Upgrading via pip\n\nTo run with the latest code for the best performance, upgrade GraphRicciCurvature to the latest version with pip:\n\n\n    pip3 install [--user] --upgrade GraphRicciCurvature\n\n\n## Getting Started\n\n  * Check the jupyter notebook tutorial on [nbviewer](https://nbviewer.jupyter.org/github/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/blob/master/notebooks/tutorial.ipynb) or [github](notebooks/tutorial.ipynb) for a walk through for the basic usage of Ricci curvature, Ricci flow, and Ricci flow for community detection.\n  * Or you can run it in directly on [binder](https://mybinder.org/v2/gh/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/master?filepath=notebooks%2Ftutorial.ipynb) (no account required) or [Google colab](https://colab.research.google.com/github/saibalmars/GraphRicciCurvature/blob/master/notebooks/tutorial.ipynb) (Faster but Google account required).\n  * Check the [Documentations](https://graphriccicurvature.readthedocs.io/en/latest/).\n  * **Try out[sample graphs](https://github.com/saibalmars/RicciFlow-SampleGraphs) with precomputed Ricci curvature/flow.**\n\n\n\n## Simple Example\n\n\n    import networkx as nx\n    from GraphRicciCurvature.OllivierRicci import OllivierRicci\n    from GraphRicciCurvature.FormanRicci import FormanRicci\n\n    print(\"\\n- Import an example NetworkX karate club graph\")\n    G = nx.karate_club_graph()\n\n    print(\"\\n===== Compute the Ollivier-Ricci curvature of the given graph G =====\")\n    # compute the Ollivier-Ricci curvature of the given graph G\n    orc = OllivierRicci(G, alpha=0.5, verbose=\"INFO\")\n    orc.compute_ricci_curvature()\n    print(\"Karate Club Graph: The Ollivier-Ricci curvature of edge (0,1) is %f\" % orc.G[0][1][\"ricciCurvature\"])\n\n    print(\"\\n===== Compute the Forman-Ricci curvature of the given graph G =====\")\n    frc = FormanRicci(G)\n    frc.compute_ricci_curvature()\n    print(\"Karate Club Graph: The Forman-Ricci curvature of edge (0,1) is %f\" % frc.G[0][1][\"formanCurvature\"])\n\n    # -----------------------------------\n    print(\"\\n=====  Compute Ricci flow metric - Optimal Transportation Distance =====\")\n    G = nx.karate_club_graph()\n    orc_OTD = OllivierRicci(G, alpha=0.5, method=\"OTD\", verbose=\"INFO\")\n    orc_OTD.compute_ricci_flow(iterations=10)\n    print(\"\\n=====  Compute Ricci community - by Ricci flow =====\")\n    clustering = orc_OTD.ricci_community()\n\n\nMore example in [example.py](example.py).\n\n* * *\n\n## Related Works\n\n  * Curvature Graph Network (ICLR2020) [openreview](https://openreview.net/forum?id=BylEqnVFDB), [code](https://github.com/yeze16159/CurvGN)\n\n\n\n## Reference\n\n[Ni]: Ni, C.-C., Lin, Y.-Y., Gao, J., Gu, X., and Saucan, E. \"Ricci curvature of the Internet topology\" (Vol. 26, pp. 2758\u20132766). {"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1712.07600.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 73878 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nComparative analysis of two discretizations of Ricci curvature for complex networks\nAreejit Samal,1, \u2217R.P. Sreejith,1, \u2217Jiao Gu,2 Shiping Liu,3 Emil Saucan,4, 5, \u2020 and J\u00a8urgen Jost6, 7, \u2021\n1The Institute of Mathematical Sciences (IMSc),\nHomi Bhabha National Institute (HBNI), Chennai 600113 India\n2Jiangnan University, Wuxi 214122 P.R. China\n3School of Mathematical Sciences, University of Science and Technology of China, Hefei 230026 P.R. China\n4Department of Applied Mathematics, ORT Braude College, Karmiel 2161002 Israel\n5Departments of Mathematics and Electrical Engineering,\nTechnion, Israel Institute of Technology, Haifa 3200003 Israel\n6Max Planck Institute for Mathematics in the Sciences, Leipzig 04103 Germany\n7The Santa Fe Institute, Santa Fe, New Mexico 87501 USA\nWe have performed an empirical comparison of two distinct notions of discrete Ricci curvature for\ngraphs or networks, namely, the Forman-Ricci curvature and Ollivier-Ricci curvature. Importantly,\nthese two discretizations of the Ricci curvature were developed based on di\ufb00erent properties of\nthe classical smooth notion, and thus, the two notions shed light on di\ufb00erent aspects of network\nstructure and behavior. Nevertheless, our extensive computational analysis in a wide range of both\nmodel and real-world networks shows that the two discretizations of Ricci curvature are highly\ncorrelated in many networks. Moreover, we show that if one considers the augmented Forman-Ricci\ncurvature which also accounts for the two-dimensional simplicial complexes arising in graphs, the\nobserved correlation between the two discretizations is even higher, especially, in real networks.\nBesides the potential theoretical implications of these observations, the close relationship between\nthe two discretizations has practical implications whereby Forman-Ricci curvature can be employed\nin place of Ollivier-Ricci curvature for faster computation in larger real-world networks whenever\ncoarse analysis su\ufb03ces.\nI.\nINTRODUCTION\nOne of the central quantities associated to a Riemannian metric is the Ricci tensor. In Einstein\u2019s \ufb01eld equations,\nthe energy-momentum tensor yields the Ricci tensor, and this determines the metric of space-time. In Riemannian\ngeometry, the importance of the Ricci tensor came to the fore in particular through the work of Gromov [1]. The\nRicci \ufb02ow, introduced by Hamilton [2], culminated in the work of Perelman [3, 4] which solved the Poincar`e and\nthe more general Geometrization Conjecture for three-dimensional manifolds. On the other hand, there have been\nimportant developments extending the notion of Ricci curvature axiomatically to metric spaces more general than\nRiemannian manifolds [5\u20137]. More precisely, one identi\ufb01es metric properties on a Riemannian manifold that can\nbe formulated in terms of local quantities such as growth of volumes of distance balls, transportation distances\nbetween balls, divergence of geodesics, and meeting probabilities of coupled random walks. On Riemannian manifolds\nsuch local quantities are implied by, or even equivalent to, Ricci curvature inequalities. Moreover when such metric\nproperties are satis\ufb01ed on some metric space, one says that the space satis\ufb01es the corresponding generalized Ricci\ncurvature inequality. This research paradigm has been remarkably successful, and the geometry of metric spaces with\nsuch inequalities is currently a very active and fertile \ufb01eld of mathematical research (see for instance [8]). Of course,\non Riemannian manifolds various such properties are equivalent to Ricci curvature inequalities and therefore also\nto each other. However, when passing to a discrete, metric setting, each approach captures di\ufb00erent aspects of the\nclassical Ricci curvature and thus, the various discretizations need no longer be equivalent. One such approach to\nRicci curvature inequalities is Ollivier\u2019s [9\u201312] construction on metric spaces.\nThere is also an older line of research [13] that searches for the discretization of Ricci curvature on graphs and\nmore general objects with a combinatorial structure. Here, one has exact quantities rather than only inequalities as\nin the aforementioned research. One elegant approach is by Chow and Luo [14] based on circle packings which lent\nitself to many practical applications in graphics, medical imaging and communication networks [15\u201317]. On the other\nhand, Ollivier\u2019s [9\u201312] discretization has proven to be suitable for modelling complex networks as well as rendering\ninteresting theoretic results with potential of future applications [18\u201324].\nYet another approach to discretization\nof Ricci curvature on polyhedral complexes, and more generally, CW complexes is due to Forman [25]. In recent\n\u2217AS and RPS contributed equally to this work\n\u2020 Corresponding author: semil@ee.technion.ac.il\n\u2021 Corresponding author: jost@mis.mpg.de\narXiv:1712.07600v2  [math.DG]  9 Jun 2018\n\n2\nwork [26\u201330], we have introduced the Forman\u2019s [25] discretization to the realm of graphs and have systematically\nexplored the Forman-Ricci curvature in complex networks. A crucial advantage of Forman-Ricci curvature is that,\nwhile it also captures important geometric properties of networks, it is far simpler to evaluate on large networks than\nOllivier-Ricci curvature [26, 30]. In this contribution, we have performed an extensive empirical comparison of the\nForman-Ricci curvature and Ollivier-Ricci curvature in complex networks. In addition, we have also performed an\nempirical analysis in complex networks of the augmented Forman-Ricci curvature which accounts for two-dimensional\nsimplicial complexes arising in graphs. We \ufb01nd that the Forman-Ricci curvature, especially the augmented version, is\nhighly correlated to Ollivier-Ricci curvature in many model and real networks. This renders Forman-Ricci curvature\na preferential tool for the analysis of very large networks with various practical applications.\nAlthough, in this contribution, we show that Forman-Ricci curvature is highly correlated to Ollivier-Ricci curvature\nin many networks, one should not construe from this observation that we introduce Forman-Ricci curvature as a sub-\nstitute (and certainly not as a \u201cproxy\u201d [31]) for Ollivier-Ricci curvature. As mentioned above, and as we shall further\nexplain in the following section, the two discretizations of Ricci curvature capture quite di\ufb00erent aspects of network\nbehavior. Indeed the speci\ufb01c de\ufb01nitions of both Ollivier\u2019s and Forman\u2019s discretizations of Ricci curvature prescribe\nsome of their respective essential properties that have important consequences in certain signi\ufb01cant applications.\nTherefore, we shall detail these de\ufb01nitions and not restrict ourselves to the mere technical de\ufb01ning formulas.\nGiven that networks permeate almost every \ufb01eld of research [32\u201338], an important challenge has been to unravel\nthe architecture of complex networks. In particular, the development of geometric tools [10, 18\u201321, 23, 24, 26, 39\u201341],\nand mainly curvature, allow us to gain deep insights into the structure, dynamics and evolution of networks. It is in\nthe very nature of discretization of di\ufb00erential geometric properties that each such discrete notion sheds a di\ufb00erent\nlight and understanding upon the studied object, for example, a network. In particular, Ollivier\u2019s curvature is related\nto clustering and network coherence via the distribution of the eigenvalues of the graph Laplacian, giving insights\ninto the global and local structure of networks. In contrast, Forman\u2019s curvature captures the geodesics dispersal\nproperty and also gives information on the algebraic topological structure of the network. Furthermore, Forman\u2019s\ncurvature is simple to compute and can easily be extended to analyze both directed networks and hyper-networks\n[26\u201329]. Given the contrast between the two discretizations of Ricci curvature at hand, the empirically observed\ncorrelation in many networks is quite surprising and encouraging. Moreover, both types of curvature admit natural\nRicci curvature \ufb02ows [16, 29] that enable the study of long time evolution and prediction of networks. Moreover, the\nobserved correlation further increases the relevance and importance of future investigation of discrete Ricci \ufb02ows for\nthe better understanding of the structure and evolution of complex networks.\nNote that in Riemannian geometry, the Ricci tensor encodes all the essential properties of a Riemannian metric.\nSimilarly, it is an emerging principle that Ricci curvature, because it evaluates edges instead of vertices, also captures\nthe basic structural aspects of a network. Both Ollivier-Ricci curvature and Forman-Ricci curvature are edge-based\nmeasures which assign a number to each edge of a (possibly weighted and directed) network that encodes local\ngeometric properties in the vicinity of that edge. We highlight that edges are what networks are made of as the\nvertices alone do not yet constitute a network.\nII.\nDISCRETE RICCI CURVATURES ON NETWORKS\nWe brie\ufb02y present here the geometric meaning of the notion of Ricci curvature, as well as the two discretizations\nconsidered herein. For other discretizations of this type of curvature and their applications, see for instance [16].\nA.\nRicci curvature\nIn Riemannian geometry curvature measures the deviation of the manifold from being locally Euclidean. Ricci\ncurvature quanti\ufb01es that deviation for tangent directions. It controls the average dispersion of geodesics around that\ndirection. It also controls the growth of the volume of distance balls and spheres. In fact, these two properties are\nrelated, as can be seen from the following formula [42]:\nVol\u03b1(\u03b5) = d\u03b1 \u03b5n\u22121\n\n1 \u2212Ric(v)\n3\n\u03b52 + o(\u03b52)\n\n.\n(1)\nHere, n is the dimension of the Riemannian manifold in question, and Vol\u03b1(\u03b5) is the (n \u22121)-volume generated within\nan n-solid angle d\u03b1 by geodesics of length \u03b5 in the direction of the vector v (i.e., it controls the growth of measured\nangles). Thus, Ricci curvature controls both divergence of geodesics and volume growth (Figure 1(a)). In dimension\nn = 2, Ricci curvature reduces to the classical Gauss curvature, and can therefore be easily visualized.\n\n3\n\u03b5\n\u03b5\nVol\u03b1(\u03b5)\nd\u03b1\n\u03bd\nP\n(a)\nS\u03b5(x)\nS\u03b5(y)\nx\ny\nWx\nWy\n\u03b3x\n\u03b3y\nmx\nmy\nx\ny\n(c)\n(d)\n(b)\nS\u03b5(x)\nS\u03b5(y)\nx\ny\nVxy\nWx\nWy\n(e)\ne\nv1\nv2\ne21\ne11\ne13\ne12\ne14\ne15\ne22\ne23\ne24\ne25\n(f)\ne\nv1\nv2\ne21\ne11\ne13\ne12\ne14\ne15\ne22\ne23\ne24\ne25\nf2\nf1\nFIG. 1. (a) The geometric interpretation of Ricci curvature. Ricci curvature measures the growth of volumes, more precisely,\nthe growth of (n \u22121)-dimensional solid angles in the direction of the vector v. It also measures the dispersion rate of the\nfamily of geodesics with the same initial point, that are contained within the given solid angle. (b)-(d) The interpretation\nof Ollivier-Ricci curvature. (b) Given two close points x and y in a Riemannian manifold of dimension n, de\ufb01ning a tangent\nvector \u00afvxy, one can consider the parallel transport in the direction \u00afvxy. Then points on a in\ufb01nitesimal sphere S\u03b5(x) centered at\nx, are transported to points on the corresponding sphere S\u03b5(y) by a distance equal to d(x, y)\n\n1 \u2212\u03b52\n2nRic(\u00afvxy)\n\n, on the average.\n(c) In Riemannian manifolds of positive (respectively, negative) curvature, balls are closer (respectively, farther) than their\ncenters. Thus, in spaces of positive Ricci curvature spheres are closer than their centers, while in spaces of negative curvature\nthey are farther away. (d) To generalize this idea to metric measure spaces, one has to replace the (volumes of) spheres or\nballs, by measures mx, my. Points will be transported by a distance equal to (1 \u2212\u03ba)d(x, y), on the average, where \u03ba = \u03ba(x, y)\nrepresents the coarse (Ollivier) curvature along the geodesic segment xy. This illustration is an adaptation of the original \ufb01gure\n[12]. (e)-(f) Forman-Ricci curvature of an edge e connecting the vertices v1 and v2 and contributions from edges parallel to\nthe edge e under consideration. An edge is said to be parallel to a given edge e, if it has in common with e either a child\n(i.e., a lower dimensional face), or a parent (i.e., a higher dimensional face), but not both simultaneously. In part (e), all\nthe edges e11, . . . , e15 are parallel to e because they share the vertex v1, while the edges e21, . . . , e25 are parallel to e because\nthey share the vertex v2. In contrast, in part (f), edges e11, e21, e15, e25 are not parallel anymore to the edge e, because they\nhave common children with e (namely, v1 and v2) and a common parent with e (namely, f1 or f2). In consequence, edges\ne11, e21, e15, e25 do not contribute in the computation of the Augmented Forman-Ricci curvature of edge e which also accounts\nfor the two-dimensional simplicial complexes f1 and f2.\n\n4\nAs we shall see, the two discretizations of Ricci curvature by Ollivier and Forman considered here for networks\ncapture di\ufb00erent properties of the classical (smooth) notion. Forman\u2019s de\ufb01nition expresses dispersal (di\ufb00usion), while\nOllivier\u2019s de\ufb01nition compares the averaged distance between balls to the distance between their centers. Thus, the\ntwo de\ufb01nitions lead to di\ufb00erent generalization of classical results regarding Ricci curvature. In this respect, Ollivier\u2019s\nversion seems to be advantageous, since, in addition to certain geometric properties, analytic inequalities also hold,\nwhereas Forman\u2019s version encapsulates mainly the topology of the underlying space.\nNevertheless, in our speci\ufb01c context of complex networks, as we shall show in the sequel, the de\ufb01nitions by Ollivier\nand Forman are highly correlated in many networks. Therefore, for the empirical analysis of large networks, at least\nin a \ufb01rst approximation, from the analysis of Forman\u2019s de\ufb01nition, one can also make inferences about the properties\nencoded by the Ollivier\u2019s de\ufb01nition. For instance, Ollivier\u2019s curvature is, by its very de\ufb01nition, excellently suited to\ncapture di\ufb00usion and stochastic properties of a given network. Unfortunately, the computation of Ollivier-Ricci cur-\nvature might be prohibitive for many large complex networks. In contrast, due to its simple, combinatorial formula,\nForman-Ricci curvature is easy and fast to compute [26]. Given the basic equivalence, at least on a statistical level,\nbetween these two discretizations, one can therefore determine, at least in \ufb01rst approximation, many properties encap-\nsulated by Ollivier\u2019s curvature via simple computations with Forman\u2019s curvature. However, for a \ufb01ner analysis, each\nof the two discrete Ricci curvatures should be employed in the context that best be\ufb01ts the geometrical phenomenology\nit encapsulates.\nB.\nOllivier-Ricci curvature\nOllivier\u2019s approach [9\u201312] interprets Eq. 1 as follows: If a small ball Bx of radius \u03b5 and centered at x is mapped,\nvia parallel transport [43] to a corresponding ball By centered at y, then the average distance between points on Bx\nand their corresponding points on By is:\n\u03b4\n\n1 \u2212\n\u03b52\n2(n + 2)Ric(v) + O(\u03b53 + \u03b52\u03b4)\n\n,\n(2)\nwhere d(x, y) = \u03b4, and where \u03b5, \u03b4 \u21920. Thus, we can synthetically characterize Ollivier-Ricci curvature [12] by the\nfollowing phrase: \u201cIn positive (negative) curvature, balls are closer (farther) than their centers are\u201d. Balls are given by\ntheir volume measures, and in fact, one may de\ufb01ne a transportation distance for any two (normalized) measures. In\nthis sense, Ollivier\u2019s notion compares the distance between the centers of their balls with that between their measures\n(Figure 1(b)-(d)). For the distance between the centers one takes (of course) the given metric of the underlying space,\ni.e., manifold, mesh, network, etc. As for the distance between measures, there is a natural choice, the Wasserstein\ntransportation metric W1 [44]. More formally, Ollivier\u2019s curvature is de\ufb01ned as:\n\u03ba(x, y) = 1 \u2212W1(mx, my)\nd(x, y)\n;\n(3)\nwhere mx, my represent the measures of the balls around x and y, respectively. Here, given that the measure m,\nassociated to the discrete set of vertices of a graph (network) is obviously a discrete measure, the Wasserstein distance\nW1(mx, my), i.e. the transportation distance between the two probability measures mx and my, is given by\nW1(mx, my) =\ninf\n\u00b5x,y\u2208Q(mx,my)\nX\n(x\u2032,y\u2032)\u2208V \u00d7V\nd(x\u2032, y\u2032)\u00b5x,y(x\u2032, y\u2032),\n(4)\nwith Q(mx, my) being the set of probability measures \u00b5x,y that satisfy:\nX\ny\u2032\u2208V\n\u00b5x,y(x\u2032, y\u2032) = mx(x\u2032),\nX\nx\u2032\u2208V\n\u00b5x,y(x\u2032, y\u2032) = my(y\u2032).\n(5)\nMeasures satisfying Eq. 5 start with the measure mx and end up with my, and represent all the transportation\npossibilities of the mass (measure) mx to the measure my, by disassembling it, transporting it, along all possible\npaths, and reassembling it as my. W1(mx, my) is the minimal cost (measured in terms of distances) to transport the\nmass of mx to that of my. Note that the distance d in Eq. 4 above can be any useful or expressive graph metric.\nHowever, in practice, when considering the Wasserstein metric and Ollivier-Ricci curvature for unweighted networks,\nthe combinatorial metric is naturally considered.\nIn the Riemannian setting, Ollivier\u2019s de\ufb01nition reduces to the classical one. More precisely, if M n is a Riemannian\nmanifold, with its natural measure dVol, then for d(x, y) small enough and v the unit tangent vector at x on the\n\n5\ngeodesic xy\n\u03ba(x, y) =\n\u03b52\n2(n + 2)Ric(v) + O(\u03b53 + \u03b52d(x, y)) .\n(6)\nThe Wasserstein distance [44] between two vertices in a network depends on the triangles, quadrangles and pentagons\nthat they are contained in (see for instance [21, 45]). It can also be computed in terms of random walks on a graph,\nwhere one has the choice between the lazy [23] and the non-lazy [21] random walk. While the two variants are clearly\nequivalent from a theoretical viewpoint, the choices may render di\ufb00erences in the implementation. In this work, we\nhave used the lazy random walk option within the open-source implementation of Ollivier-Ricci curvature, originally\ndeveloped by P. Romon and improved by E. Madsen, within SageMath software (http://www.sagemath.org/) for our\ncomputations.\nWhile Ollivier-Ricci curvature is essentially de\ufb01ned on edges, one can de\ufb01ne Ollivier-Ricci curvature of a vertex [24]\nas the sum of the Ollivier-Ricci curvatures of edges incident on that vertex in the network, and this is analogous to\nscalar curvature in Riemannian geometry [43].\nC.\nForman-Ricci curvature\nForman\u2019s de\ufb01nition is conceptually quite di\ufb00erent from Ollivier\u2019s de\ufb01nition. To begin with, Forman\u2019s de\ufb01nition\nworks in the framework of weighted CW cell complexes, rather than that of Markov chains and metric measure\nspaces, as Ollivier\u2019s de\ufb01nition does. The weighted CW cell complexes are of fundamental importance in topology\nand include both polygonal meshes and weighted graphs. In the setting of weighted CW cell complexes, Forman\u2019s\nde\ufb01nition develops an abstract version of a classical formula in di\ufb00erential geometry or geometric analysis, the so\ncalled Bochner-Weitzenb\u00a8ock formula (see for instance [43]), that relates curvature to the classical (Riemannian)\nLaplace operator.\nForman [25] derived an analogue of the Bochner-Weitzenb\u00a8ock formula that holds in the setting of CW complexes.\nIn the 1-dimensional case, i.e. of graphs or networks, it takes the following form [26]:\nF(e) = we\n\uf8eb\n\uf8edwv1\nwe\n+ wv2\nwe\n\u2212\nX\nev1 \u223ce, ev2 \u223ce\n\"\nwv1\n\u221awewev1\n+\nwv2\n\u221awewev2\n#\uf8f6\n\uf8f8\n(7)\nwhere e denotes the edge under consideration between two nodes v1 and v2, we denotes the weight of the edge e\nunder consideration, wv1 and wv2 denote the weights associated with the vertices v1 and v2, respectively, ev1 \u223ce\nand ev2 \u223ce denote the set of edges incident on vertices v1 and v2, respectively, after excluding the edge e under\nconsideration which connects the two vertices v1 and v2 (Figure 1(e)). Since edges in the discrete setting of networks\nnaturally correspond to vectors or directions in the smooth context, the above formula represents, in view of the\nclassical Bochner-Weitzenb\u00a8ock formula, a discretization of Ricci curvature. For gaining further intuition regarding\nthis discretization of Ricci curvature in its generality, the reader is referred to Forman\u2019s original work [25], and to our\nprevious papers [26, 30] for"}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://arxiv.org/pdf/1712.07600.pdf\" --max-chars 20000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "aefd5039b0fd", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/.oh_sessions/4aa7348867f64e409f0a4d14a0cd32a3/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

   0.5.3.1\n\n\n### Load sample graph\u00b6\n\n  * First, let\u2019s load karate club graph from networkx as an example.\n  * Ricci curavture and Ricci flow supported weighted graph, but for simplification, let\u2019s start from the unweighted graph first.\n\n\n\n\n    [3]:\n\n\n\n    G = nx.karate_club_graph()\n    for (n1, n2, d) in G.edges(data=True):\n        d.clear()   # remove edge weight\n\n\n\n    [4]:\n\n\n\n    print(G)\n\n\n\n    Graph named \"Zachary's Karate Club\" with 34 nodes and 78 edges\n\n\n* * *\n\n## Discrete Ricci curvature\u00b6\n\nCurvature is a geometric property to describe the local shape of an object. In the graph, an edge with _positive curvature_ represents an edge within a cluster, while a _negative curvature_ edge tents to be a bridge within clusters.\n\nCurrently, there are multiple ways to discretize curvature on graph, in this library, we include two of the most frequently used discrete Ricci curvature:\n\n  * **Ollivier-Ricci curvature** : Optimal transportation theory-based curvature. Provide a micro view of curvature for each edge but more computational heavy.\n  * **Forman-Ricci curvature** : CW complexes based curvature. Provide a macro view of curvature for each edge with super light computation speed.\n\n\n\nNotice that while these two curvatures are shown to have a similar trend [[samal]](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-018-27001-3), they are defined from different point fo views and should be treated as different property.\n\n### Ollivier-Ricci curvature\u00b6\n\n  * To compute the Ollivier-Ricci curvature of a graph, we can use the class `OllivierRicci` to load the graph and setup the parameter for the curvature computation.\n\n\n\n\n    [5]:\n\n\n\n    orc = OllivierRicci(G, alpha=0.5, verbose=\"TRACE\")\n\n\n\n    INFO:Edge weight not detected in graph, use \"weight\" as default edge weight.\n\n\n  * After setup the class `orc`, we can call `compute_ricci_curvature` to start the Ricci curvature computation. The graph in the class with results will be updated.\n\n\n\n\n    [6]:\n\n\n\n    orc.compute_ricci_curvature()\n    G_orc = orc.G.copy()  # save an intermediate result\n\n\n\n    TRACE:Number of nodes: 34\n    TRACE:Number of edges: 78\n    TRACE:Start to compute all pair shortest path.\n    TRACE:0.000765 secs for all pair by NetworKit.\n    INFO:0.054517 secs for Ricci curvature computation.\n\n\n  * The computed edge Ricci curvature is then stored in the returned networkx graph `G_orc`. Let\u2019s check the first five results and plot the histogram of the edge Ricci curvature distributions.\n  * In most of case, Ollivier-Ricci curvatures are in the range of [-1, 1].\n  * The Ricci curvature distributions is shown to be different from graph to graph, and can be act as a graph fingerprint or graph kernel.\n\n\n\n\n    [7]:\n\n\n\n    def show_results(G, curvature=\"ricciCurvature\"):\n\n        # Print the first five results\n        print(\"Karate Club Graph, first 5 edges: \")\n        for n1,n2 in list(G.edges())[:5]:\n            print(\"Ricci curvature of edge (%s,%s) is %f\" % (n1 ,n2, G[n1][n2][curvature]))\n\n        # Plot the histogram of Ricci curvatures\n        plt.subplot(2, 1, 1)\n        ricci_curvtures = nx.get_edge_attributes(G, curvature).values()\n        plt.hist(ricci_curvtures,bins=20)\n        plt.xlabel('Ricci curvature')\n        plt.title(\"Histogram of Ricci Curvatures (Karate Club)\")\n\n        # Plot the histogram of edge weights\n        plt.subplot(2, 1, 2)\n        weights = nx.get_edge_attributes(G, \"weight\").values()\n        plt.hist(weights,bins=20)\n        plt.xlabel('Edge weight')\n        plt.title(\"Histogram of Edge weights (Karate Club)\")\n\n        plt.tight_layout()\n\n    show_results(G_orc)\n\n\n\n    Karate Club Graph, first 5 edges:\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,1) is 0.111111\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,2) is -0.143750\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,3) is 0.041667\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,4) is -0.114583\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,5) is -0.281250\n\n\n### Forman-Ricci curvature\u00b6\n\n  * To compute the Forman-Ricci curvature of a graph, we can use the class `FormanRicci` to load the graph and setup the parameter for the curvature computation.\n\n\n\n\n    [8]:\n\n\n\n    frc = FormanRicci(G, verbose=\"TRACE\")\n\n\n\n    INFO:Edge weight not detected in graph, use \"weight\" as default edge weight.\n    INFO:Node weight not detected in graph, use \"weight\" as default node weight.\n\n\n  * As before, after setup the class `frc`, we can call `compute_ricci_curvature` to start the Ricci curvature computation.\n\n\n\n\n    [9]:\n\n\n\n    frc.compute_ricci_curvature()\n    G_frc = frc.G.copy()  # save an intermediate result\n\n\n  * Notice that the Forman-Ricci curvature have different curvature distribution compare with Ollivier-Ricci curvature since they are defined from two different angles.\n  * Forman-Ricci curvature is not bounded. In this graph, the curvatures are ranged in [-20, 5].\n\n\n\n\n    [10]:\n\n\n\n    show_results(G_frc,curvature=\"formanCurvature\")\n\n\n\n    Karate Club Graph, first 5 edges:\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,1) is 0.000000\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,2) is -7.000000\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,3) is -3.000000\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,4) is -9.000000\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,5) is -10.000000\n\n\n\n    [10]:\n\n\n\n\n\n## Ricci flow\u00b6\n\n  * Ricci flow is an iterative process that aims to smooth out the curvatures of the input graph by adjusting the edges weight, it stretches edges of large negative Ricci curvature and shrinks edges of large positive Ricci curvature over time.\n  * To compute the Ollivier-Ricci flow, simply call function `compute_ricci_flow` to start the flow process. The iterations of flow is controlled by variable `iterations`.\n\n\n\n\n    [11]:\n\n\n\n    # Start a Ricci flow with Lin-Yau's probability distribution setting with 4 process.\n    orf = OllivierRicci(G, alpha=0.5, base=1, exp_power=0, proc=4, verbose=\"INFO\")\n\n    # Do Ricci flow for 2 iterations\n    orf.compute_ricci_flow(iterations=2)\n\n\n\n    INFO:Edge weight not detected in graph, use \"weight\" as default edge weight.\n    INFO:No ricciCurvature detected, compute original_RC...\n    INFO:0.021153 secs for Ricci curvature computation.\n    INFO: === Ricci flow iteration 0 ===\n    INFO:0.018483 secs for Ricci curvature computation.\n    INFO: === Ricci flow iteration 1 ===\n    INFO:0.017849 secs for Ricci curvature computation.\n    INFO:0.068870 secs for Ricci flow computation.\n\n\n\n    [11]:\n\n\n\n    <networkx.classes.graph.Graph at 0x1778cbac0>\n\n\n  * After \u201cenough\u201d Ricci flow iterations, the Ricci curvature of the graph will be converged to some value. In our experience, most of graphs need around 20~50 iterations.\n  * Now let\u2019s do more Ricci flow to flatten the Ricci curvature and refine the Ricci flow metric.\n\n\n\n\n    [12]:\n\n\n\n    orf.set_verbose(\"ERROR\") # mute logs\n    orf.compute_ricci_flow(iterations=50)\n    G_rf = orf.G.copy()\n\n\n  * After Ricci flow, the edge weights (Ricci flow metrics) changed and the edge Ricci curvatures are converged to \\\\(-0.0115\\\\).\n\n\n\n\n    [13]:\n\n\n\n    show_results(G_rf)\n\n\n\n    Karate Club Graph, first 5 edges:\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,1) is -0.002814\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,2) is -0.002783\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,3) is -0.002808\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,4) is -0.002823\n    Ricci curvature of edge (0,5) is -0.002830\n\n\n\n    [13]:\n\n\n\n\n\n## Ricci flow for Community detection\u00b6\n\nIn this section, we will show you how to use (Ollivier-) Ricci flow metric to detect community. If you wish to know how to use the build-in function directly, please jump to `Ricci community` section.\n\n### Preliminary\u00b6\n\n#### Visualized the communities\u00b6\n\n  * We can apply Ricci flow to detect communities in graph.\n  * To visualized the communities in graph, let\u2019s first draw the graph and color the nodes with its community.\n\n\n\n\n    [14]:\n\n\n\n    def draw_graph(G, clustering_label=\"club\"):\n        \"\"\"\n        A helper function to draw a nx graph with community.\n        \"\"\"\n        complex_list = nx.get_node_attributes(G, clustering_label)\n\n        le = preprocessing.LabelEncoder()\n        node_color = le.fit_transform(list(complex_list.values()))\n\n        nx.draw_spring(G, nodelist=G.nodes(),\n                       node_color=node_color,\n                       cmap=plt.cm.rainbow,\n                       alpha=0.8)\n\n    draw_graph(G_rf)\n\n\n#### Detect the communities\u00b6\n\n  * To detect the communities of the graph, we can try a simple but effected method: **remove all edges with weight greater than a threshold**.\n  * By observing the histogram of edge weights, let\u2019s set the threshold to be 1.5 or 1.0, and apply modularity and Adjust Rand Index(ARI) as clustering metrics to evaluate the clustering result.\n    * _Modularity_ : A clustering metrics define **without ground-truth**.\n    * _ARI_ : A clustering metrics define **with ground-truth**.\n\n\n\n\n    [15]:\n\n\n\n    def ARI(G, clustering, clustering_label=\"club\"):\n        \"\"\"\n        Computer the Adjust Rand Index (clustering accuracy) of \"clustering\" with \"clustering_label\" as ground truth.\n\n        Parameters\n        ----------\n        G : NetworkX graph\n            A given NetworkX graph with node attribute \"clustering_label\" as ground truth.\n        clustering : dict or list or list of set\n            Predicted community clustering.\n        clustering_label : str\n            Node attribute name for ground truth.\n\n        Returns\n        -------\n        ari : float\n            Adjust Rand Index for predicted community.\n        \"\"\"\n\n        complex_list = nx.get_node_attributes(G, clustering_label)\n\n        le = preprocessing.LabelEncoder()\n        y_true = le.fit_transform(list(complex_list.values()))\n\n        if isinstance(clustering, dict):\n            # python-louvain partition format\n            y_pred = np.array([clustering[v] for v in complex_list.keys()])\n        elif isinstance(clustering[0], set):\n            # networkx partition format\n            predict_dict = {c: idx for idx, comp in enumerate(clustering) for c in comp}\n            y_pred = np.array([predict_dict[v] for v in complex_list.keys()])\n        elif isinstance(clustering, list):\n            # sklearn partition format\n            y_pred = clustering\n        else:\n            return -1\n\n        return metrics.adjusted_rand_score(y_true, y_pred)\n\n\n\n    def my_surgery(G_origin: nx.Graph(), weight=\"weight\", cut=0):\n        \"\"\"A simple surgery function that remove the edges with weight above a threshold\n\n        Parameters\n        ----------\n        G_origin : NetworkX graph\n            A graph with ``weight`` as Ricci flow metric to cut.\n        weight: str\n            The edge weight used as Ricci flow metric. (Default value = \"weight\")\n        cut: float\n            Manually assigned cutoff point.\n\n        Returns\n        -------\n        G : NetworkX graph\n            A graph after surgery.\n        \"\"\"\n        G = G_origin.copy()\n        w = nx.get_edge_attributes(G, weight)\n\n        assert cut >= 0, \"Cut value should be greater than 0.\"\n        if not cut:\n            cut = (max(w.values()) - 1.0) * 0.6 + 1.0  # Guess a cut point as default\n\n        to_cut = []\n        for n1, n2 in G.edges():\n            if G[n1][n2][weight] > cut:\n                to_cut.append((n1, n2))\n        print(\"*************** Surgery time ****************\")\n        print(\"* Cut %d edges.\" % len(to_cut))\n        G.remove_edges_from(to_cut)\n        print(\"* Number of nodes now: %d\" % G.number_of_nodes())\n        print(\"* Number of edges now: %d\" % G.number_of_edges())\n        cc = list(nx.connected_components(G))\n        print(\"* Modularity now: %f \" % nx.algorithms.community.quality.modularity(G, cc))\n        print(\"* ARI now: %f \" % ARI(G, cc))\n        print(\"*********************************************\")\n\n        return G\n\n\n\n    [16]:\n\n\n\n    draw_graph(my_surgery(G_rf, cut=1.5))\n\n\n\n    *************** Surgery time ****************\n    * Cut 12 edges.\n    * Number of nodes now: 34\n    * Number of edges now: 66\n    * Modularity now: 0.063117\n    * ARI now: 0.072402\n    *********************************************\n\n\n\n    [17]:\n\n\n\n    draw_graph(my_surgery(G_rf, cut=1.0))\n\n\n\n    *************** Surgery time ****************\n    * Cut 37 edges.\n    * Number of nodes now: 34\n    * Number of edges now: 41\n    * Modularity now: 0.687396\n    * ARI now: 0.321943\n    *********************************************\n\n\n  * Both of the above cutoffs are not that promising. It seems to be a lot of works to find a good cutoff point, let\u2019s try to automize it by scan through the possible cutoff points and plot the corresponding accuracy.\n\n\n\n\n    [18]:\n\n\n\n    def check_accuracy(G_origin, weight=\"weight\", clustering_label=\"value\", plot_cut=True):\n        \"\"\"To check the clustering quality while cut the edges with weight using different threshold\n\n        Parameters\n        ----------\n        G_origin : NetworkX graph\n            A graph with ``weight`` as Ricci flow metric to cut.\n        weight: float\n            The edge weight used as Ricci flow metric. (Default value = \"weight\")\n        clustering_label : str\n            Node attribute name for ground truth.\n        plot_cut: bool\n            To plot the good guessed cut or not.\n\n        \"\"\"\n        G = G_origin.copy()\n        modularity, ari = [], []\n        maxw = max(nx.get_edge_attributes(G, weight).values())\n        cutoff_range = np.arange(maxw, 1, -0.025)\n\n        for cutoff in cutoff_range:\n            edge_trim_list = []\n            for n1, n2 in G.edges():\n                if G[n1][n2][weight] > cutoff:\n                    edge_trim_list.append((n1, n2))\n            G.remove_edges_from(edge_trim_list)\n\n            # Get connected component after cut as clustering\n            clustering = {c: idx for idx, comp in enumerate(nx.connected_components(G)) for c in comp}\n\n            # Compute modularity and ari\n            modularity.append(community_louvain.modularity(clustering, G, weight))\n            ari.append(ARI(G, clustering, clustering_label=clustering_label))\n\n        plt.xlim(maxw, 0)\n        plt.xlabel(\"Edge weight cutoff\")\n        plt.plot(cutoff_range, modularity, alpha=0.8)\n        plt.plot(cutoff_range, ari, alpha=0.8)\n\n        if plot_cut:\n            good_cut = -1\n            mod_last = modularity[-1]\n            drop_threshold = 0.01  # at least drop this much to considered as a drop for good_cut\n\n            # check drop from 1 -> maxw\n            for i in range(len(modularity) - 1, 0, -1):\n                mod_now = modularity[i]\n                if mod_last > mod_now > 1e-4 and abs(mod_last - mod_now) / mod_last > drop_threshold:\n                    if good_cut != -1:\n                        print(\"Other cut:%f, diff:%f, mod_now:%f, mod_last:%f, ari:%f\" % (\n                            cutoff_range[i + 1], mod_last - mod_now, mod_now, mod_last, ari[i + 1]))\n                    else:\n                        good_cut = cutoff_range[i + 1]\n                        print(\""}], "is_error": false, "command": "export SKILL_DIR=\"/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://graphriccicurvature.readthedocs.io/en/latest/tutorial.html\" --max-chars 20000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "aefd5039b0fd", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_yVP3EQYR2wGM/.oh_sessions/4aa7348867f64e409f0a4d14a0cd32a3/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}
